FOREWORD

The Subcommlttee on Radlochemlstry is one of a number of
subcommlttees working under the Committee on Nuclear Science
within the National Academy of Sclences - National Research
Councll. Its members represent government, industrial, and
universlty laboratories In the areas of nuclear chemistry and
analytlcal chemlstry

The Subcommittee has concerned 1tseelf with those areas of
nuclear sclence which Involve the chemist, such as the collec-
tion and dlstributlion of radlochemlcal procedures, the estab-
lishment of speciflcations for radlochemically pure reagents,
avallabllity of cyclotron time for service irradiations, the
place of radiochemlstry in the undergraduate college program,
etc.

This serles of monographs has grown out of the need for
up-to~date compllations of radiochemical information and pro-
cedures. The Subcommlttee has endeavored to present a series
which wlll be of maximum use to the working sclentlst and
which contalne the latest avallable informatlon. Each mono-
graph collects In one volume the pertinent information required
for radiochemical work with an individual element or a group of
closely related elements.

Anh expert in the radlochemlatry of the particular element
has written the monograph, following a standsrd format developed
by the Subcommittee. The Atomlc Energy Commission has sponsored
the printing of the serles.

The Subcommittee 1s confident these publications willl be
useful not only to the radiochemlet but also to the research
worker in other flelds such as physics, blochemlistry or medlcilne
who wishes to use radlochemical techniques to solve a speclflc
problem.

W. Wayne Meinke, Chalrman
Subcommittee on Radlochemistry

INTRODUCTION

This volume which deals with the radiochemlstry of
uranium 1s one of a series of monographs on radlochemlstry
of the elements. There 1s Included a review of the nuclear
and chemlcal features of partlcular interest to the radio-
chemist, a discusslon of problems of dlssolutlon of a sample
end counting technlques; and flnally, a collectlon of radlo-
chemical procedures for the element as found 1n the lltera-
ture.

The series of monographs wlll cover all elements for
whlch radiochemlcal procedures are pertinent. Plans include
revision of the monograph perlodically as new techniques and
procedures warrant. The reader ls therefore encouraged to
call to the attentlon of the author any published or unpub-
lished material on the radlochemlistry of uranium which mlght
be included in a revised version of the monograph.

IV. Revlew of Those Features of Uranium Chemistry of Chief

Interest to the Radlochemilst.

A. Metalllec Uranium

1. Preparation. Uranium metal may be prepared by several methods:
the reduction of uranium oxldes wlth carbon in an arc-melting
furnace; reduction of uranlum oxides wlth magneslum, aluminum,
calcium or calclum hydride; the reductlon of uranium halides
wlth alkall or alkaline-earth metals; electrolytic reduction
of uranium halides; and the thermal decomposition of uranium.

iodide.
2. Physical properties. Metallle uranium exlsts In three allo-
tropic forms 223 the orthorhomblec alpha form, stable below
663°C; the tetragonal beta form which exists between 663°C and
770°C; and the body-centered cublc form which exlsta at higher
temperatures (> 770°C). The physical properties of the metal
as complled by Grainger are given in Table I. Because of the
method of preparation, impurities may be contained 1n the

metal which alter 1ts properties. Also, a number of the physi-
cal characteristics depend upon anisotropic and structural
effects, eg. thermal expansion. Therefore, 1f physical proper-
tles are pertinent to an experiment or design, 1t is best to
determine them individually for the metal used.

The changes wrought in metalllc uranium by radietion and

thermal cycling may be considerable. The results of reactor radla-

tion on the metal are: dimensional instability, surface roughening

and pimpling, warping, high hardness, extreme brittleness, cracks

and porosity, broadened x-ray diffraction lines, and decreased

3

thermal and electrical conductlvity.= Thermal cycling growth ls

similar in many respects to that caused by radiation damage.

However, dlfferences exlst, the fundamental difference belng

in the mechanism of growth. (The reader 1s directed to reference

3 for more detalled discussion of this subject.)

3. Chemical properties. Uranium 18 & highly reactlve metal, A

potentlal of +1.80 volts for the half-cell reaction, U- ut3 + 3e,

places 1t below beryllium and above hafnium and aluminum in the

electromotive force series.2 The metal forms lntermetallic

compounds with Al, Be, Bi, Co, Cu, Ga, Au, Fe, Pb, Mn, Hg, Ni,

Sn,
zr,

N,,

Ge, In, Ir, Pd, Pt, Tl, and zn;2 80l1d solutlons with Mo, Ti,
and Nb.2 Tt reacts at varying temperatures with H,, B, C, 51,

P, As, O
1,2

NOs CH, Co, CO, =’= In alr, at room temperature, massive

uranlum tarnlshes to form a yellow and. eventually a black oxide

ps Ss Se, Fy, Cly, Bry, I,, Hy0, HF, H,S, NH, NO, HO,
coating. Finely divided powder may burn spontaneously. In bolling
water, massive uranlum corrodes slowly wlth the formation of uran-
lum dioxlde and hydrogen. The reaction products with steam are
uranlum oxide and hydride. The diseolutlon of uranium metal 1s

discussed 1n sectlon IV-F.

IV-B. Compounds of Uranium

Uranium comblnes with most elements to form a large number
and varlety of compounds. "Gmeline Handbuch der Anorganischen
Chemie, "© which surveys the literature through the year 1935,
describes several hundred compounds. Katz and Seaborgs describe
gome of the more recently prepared compounds, principally of
organlc character, such as chelates, alkoxldes, amides, mercap-

tides, and w-cyclopentadienyl compounds.
The oxldatlion states of uranium in the comblned form vary
from II to VI. Divalent uranium compounds reported are UO and
US. Trivalent uranium compounds are more numerous and include
the hydride, nitride, seaqulsulfilde, halides and borohydride.
Uranium (III) sulfate UH(80,), has also been reported.L a
large number of tetravalent compounds are known varylng in
complexlty from the oxide and simple blnary salts to more com-
plicated organic structures. Complex salts such as 3(0N3Hg) C04 +

u(coy) 41,0 and 2(NH,),C 0, . U(C,0y), . 6H,0 form an impor-

5 .
tant group of uranium (IV) compounds. Complex salts are formed
also with heallde, sulfite, sulfate, and phosphate lone. Inorgenic

compounds of pentavelent uranium are UF5, UClg, UCl, . S0C1,, 8

UClg . po1, 2 and UF ’ xuF, 8 U0Cl4 has been reported as an lnter-
medlate compound in the chlorination of uranium oxldes wlth

carbon tetrachloride.t Uranium (V) alkoxides have been pre-
pared.2 Also, the compounds (CgHgN), UOClg and UOCl, - EtOH

have been reported. Hexavalent uranlum ls represented by

UFg, UClg, Uo, uranates, and uranyl (vost) compounds. Uranyl
compounds are the moet numerous uranium compounds and vary in

type from simple salte to complex organic arrangements. Complex
salts are formed with halide, lodate, nitrate, carbonate, cyanide,
acetate, oxalate, sulfate, phosphate, arsenate, chromate and
vanadate lons. Triple acetate salts of the form

wut (v0,),4(CHC0,) + 6H,0,
where M' 18 an alkali metal (Li, Na, or XK) and M'! is a divalent
metal (Mg, N1, Zn, etec.), are used in analytical separations of
uranium. Addition compounds, such as uo, (No

represent a large number of uranyl compounds.

Uranates and peruranates are important in the analytical

chemistry of uranium. Uranates have the general formula AMZ0 .

yUO4 or lo . yUO5. They may be prepared by different methods. 219

However, in usual analytlcel procedures, they are precipitated
from a uranyl solutlon by the addition of a soluable metal hydrox-
ide, NH,, OH, NaOH, Ca(OH), etc. The uranates are insoluble in
water but dissolve ln acids.

Peruranates® are formed when uranyl solutions containing
hydrogen peroxide are made alkaline. The composltlion of the
peruranates depends upon the concentration of the slkall and
peroxide. The following groups have been 1ldentifled:

MyU,014 - xH,0, M,UOg . xH,0, MgUs0q3 . xH,0, and M, UOg . XH,0.
The peruranates are generally soluble in water. The least aoluble
are those of the M;U50:0 . xH,0 group. The peruranetes are
soluble in dilute mineral acids.

hexapositive states. Ions 1n these states are usually repre-

Triposltive uranium, ut3, Evidence for the existence of

 

U3 comes from the reversibility of the U(III)/U(IV) couple.
Solutions mey be prepared by the dissolution of a uranium
trihalide or by the electrolytic reduction of a uranium (IV)
or (VI) solution. Chloride, bromide, 1lodide, perchlorate

and sulfate solutions of uranium(III) have been reported.il
They are deep red in color and unstable, with oxidation of gt3
to AR occurring and hydrogen being evolved. Strongly acidi-
fled solutions:2 or those kept at low temperatures: appear
to be more stable.

Tetrapositive uranium, gH, The existence of the TU

+

acetylacetone. it Uranous solutlons may be prepered by dis-
solutlon of a water-soluble salt: the chloride, bramide,
lodide, or sulfate; by dissolutlonh of uranium or a uranium
compound in an appropriate solvent, e.g., uranium metal in
sulfuric or phosphoric acid; or by reduction of a uranyl
solution by chemical, electrochemical or photochemical means.
The solutions are green in color. They are stable in the
absence of alr but are oxidized by oxygen. Uranium(IV) under-

Hexapositlve uranium, vos? . A number of physical-chemil-

 

cal measurements as well as crystallographlec, infrared and
Raman spectra studles support the existence of U(VI) ion as
vo}. 2.210 Uranyl solutions are easily prepared by dls-
solution of water-soluble salts: the nitrate, fluoride,
chloride, bromlde, lodlde, sulfate, and acetate. Other water-
soluble uranyl salts include those of other organic acids:
the formate, propionate, butyrate, and valerate; and certain
double salts such as potasslum uranyl sulfate, sodlum uranyl

may be prepared also by dissolution of a uranium(VI) compound
in an appropriate solvent, by dissolution of a lower valence
uranium compound in an oxldizlng medium, or by oxldatlon

of lower valence uranium lons already ln solution. Uranyl

solutions are yellow 1n color. They are the most stable of

uranium solutions. As indicated 1n preceding paragraphe, the

19
uranyl ion may be reduced by reducing agentsst or by electro-
chemical or photochemical means. The degree of dissociation
of uranyl salts ln aqueous solution varies. Uranyl perchlorate
1s apparently completely dissociated ;22 whereas, uranyl
fluoride 18 undlssoclated and tends to form dimers (see section
on complex ion formation - Tv-cp).35.36 Hydrolysis of the
uranyl lon has been the subject of extensive 1nvestigatlon.
Conailderable evidence has been adduced for the formation of
polymeric species of the type vo, (U0,) 2+. 37=AT According

to sutton, 2 formation of polymers beyond the trimer U405°"
is negligible. However, the trimer itself mey undergo
further hydrolysie with the formation of u50g (0H) ", U;0g (0H) ,,
and eventually anlonlec species. 10.41 Ahrland, 2 in his
original paper, proposed the formation of the monomer vo, (oH) *
as well as polynuclear species. In a reappraisal of the

work, Arhland, Hletanen, and s111%n3 stated that there was
no certain indication of mononuclear complexes being formed.
Rather, the experlmental data was explained on the basis

that complex lons of the type v0, [ (0H) 00, 15% were formed.
From the data it was not possible to distingulsh between a
limited mechanism in which n varied from 1 to 3 or 4 or an
unlimited mechanism ln whlch n assumed all 1ntegral values.
The authors were 1lnclined to prefer the latter. Kraus:
suggested that reactlons leading to the formation of polymers
may have a less positive value of AH than the reactlon
leading to the formation of the monomer. Consequently, the
latter process might be 1ldentified more readily at high
temperatures than at room temperature. This 1s apparently
the case as was shown by Hearne and Whitetl who determined

the enthalpy change to be 20.8 kcal/mole for the monomeric
reaction (vo 0H" formed) and 6.7 kcal/mole for the dimeric

+

IV-D Separation of Uranium

 

A number of review articles have been written on the analyti-
cal chemistry of uranium.8:3%,189-200 These, together with many
texts on chemical analysis (see, for example, references 201-209),
serve well as guldes to the separation and puriflcatlon of the ele-
ment. More specialized surveys have been made by Hecht222 on the
quantitative mlcro-analysis of uranium-bearing minerals, and
by Lawrowsk1ZE on separation processes for the recovery of
nuclear fuels.

Two general techniques are avallable for the separation of

uranium. (1) Uranium 18 removed from solution 1n the presence

39
of contaminants by precipitation, solvent extraction or some
alternative method. (2) Uranium is kept in solution and contam-
inants are removed. These techniques are facllltated by the
fact that uranium 1s reasonably stable ln two oxidation states,
(Iv) and (VI), and that complex formation mey be effected to
prevent the removal of either uranium or contaminant from solution.
In the followlng paragraphs, the eeparatlon of uranium by
precipitatlon, solvent extraction, and ion exchange are described
1n gome detall. Reference 1s made also to other methods of separa-
tion: chromatography, electrodeposition, volatilization and

pyrometallurgy.

1. Precipitation. In classlcal systems of analyels, uranium
1s 8a member of the third group of elements. | That 1s,
1t 1s not precipitated by hydrochloric acld or by hydrogen
sulfide in an acidic solution, but 1t 1s preclpltated by
ammonium hydroxide or ammonium sulfide (see references 204,
206, 208, 213). Unfortunately, for the separation of
uranium, many other elements also are preclpltated by the
same reagents. However, there exlsts a large number of
reagents capable of preclpltating uranium over a wilde range
of pH. These, combined wlth Judlclous use of the two oxidation
states and/or the complexing ability of uranium, may be used
to provlde reasonably pure uranium deposits.

Precipitants. With the advent of nuclear energy as a
source of power, numerous preclpitants have been investl-
gated 1n an effort to find one specific for the separation
and/or determination of uranium. None have been found to

date. Ware21t has summarized early work using organic rea-

Carriers. Trace amounts of uranium may be removed from
solution by the use of gathering agente or carriers. The
cholce of a particular agent depends upon the conditions
under which precipitation 1s to be made and upon subsequent
chemistry to whlch the precipltate is to be subjected.
Rodden and ware3t have deseribed the applicatlon of several
carriers: ferric, aluminum, and calclum hydroxide. The use
of barlum carbonate and thorium hexametaphosphate has been
mentioned in the section on 1lnorganic precipltants. Mag-
nesium oxlde and thorium psroxide have been used. 32 The
oxlde and salts of antimony, 282.282 calcium fluoride,3t
and the phosphates of zirconium, +27 bismuth,283 and thoriumt2ls
240 have been used to carry uranium from reduced solutions.
Uranium (IV), in general, should behave simllarly as nep-
tunium (IV) and plutonium (IV). These are carried by
lanthanum fluoride, cerlc and zlrconium lodates, ceric and
thorium oxalates, barium sulfate, zlrconlum phosphate, and
bismuth grsonate. 218 Uranium (VI) does not carry wlth these
agents providing the concentration of either carrler or

uranium 1s not too large.

Complexes. The preclpitation of uranium in normally

 

precipltating medla ie inhibited by the formation of

soluble complexes. 3% Carbonate lon 1B a very efficient
complexing agent of uranyl lon. In ammonium hydroxlde

solution, uranium can be separated from iron, titanium,
zirconium, and aluminum with carbonate lon present. In ammonium

sulfide solutions, carbonate lon makes possible the separation

58
of uranium from manganese, lron, cobalt, zinc, and titanium.
Ammonium carbonate prevents the precilpltation of uranium with
phosphate. Preclplitation wlth sodium carbonate makes possible
the separation of uranium from beryllium, manganese, iron, cobalt,
nickel, zine, titanium, zlrconlum, and the alkaline earths.

SodIum peroxide facllltates the separation of uranium and
other metals with sodlum carbonate. The addition of the per-
oxide alone to acid solutions of iron, cobalt, rare earths,
titanium, zirconium, hafnium, and thorium causes thelr preci-
pitation while uranium, 1f present, remains in solution.

Uranium does not precipitate with tannic acld in slightly
acldic solution wlth oxalate lon present. Titanium, nlobium,
tin, tantalum, and tungsten are preclpitated under such con-

of uranium by ammonia.

Solvent extraction. The solubllity of uranyl nitrate in
organic solvents has long been recognized.182 The ability
of dlethyl ether to extract this salt has been used in
systems of anelysls for many years. However, 1t 1s only
within recent years (starting in the 1940's) that widespread
use has been made of solvent technlques as a means of
separating and purlfylng inorganic substances in general 289-299
and uranium in particular, 12L:192,19%4,197-199, 300-305

The conditlons under which uranium may be extracted are
many and varled. In the present paper, extraction from

aqueous solutlon 1s considered. However, extraction from

solid phases322-397 and slurries3®2 has been investigated

60
and a favorable uranium partition has been found. Conditions
whlch affect the extraction of uranium from aqueous solutlon
by organic solvent are the composition of the aqueous phase,
the nature of the organic phase, the temperature, and the
time of equllibration. In the aqueous phase, such factors
ag uranium, acld, common anion, forelgn anlon, and foreign
cation concentration must be considered. The nature of the
organic phase depends upon the type and concentratlon of
solvent and dlluent. If the organic phase 1s not 1nitlally
barren, lte concentration of uranium, acld, etc., affects
partition.

Because of the number of variables and the large number
of uranlum solvents, one cannot conelder, in a volume of this
glze, each solvent in the light of each variable. Indeed,
the behavioral relation between solvent and the afore-men-
tioned varlables 18 known for only a few well-studled solvents.
The purpose of the present paper 18 to provide information
on the conditions best-sulted for the quantitative extraction
of uranium or for the separation of uranium from interfering
elements. This 18 done as much as possible in graphic or
tabular form.

The solvents are divided into flve general classiflcatlions:
1) ethers, esters, ketones, and alcohols; 2) organo-phosphorous
compounds; 3) amines and quaternary emmonium salts; I) carboxylic
aclds; 5) chelating agents. Dialkylphosphorlc aclds, eg.,
dibutyl phosphate, are classified as organophosphorus com-
pounds rather than chelating agents. Carboxylic acide are
classifled a8 Buch, although some may also be consldered
chelating agents, eg., sallcyllc acid. A number of extrac-
tants may serve also as diluents or secondary solvents for
other extractants. Such systems are described under the
primary extractant. For example, & cupferron-hexone system

is described under "cupferron'" rather than under "hexone!

61

a secondary solvent rather than the term "lnert solvent." The
cholce of dlluent may appreclably affect the partition of
uranium. A number of terms that are frequently used are
defined below.

Extractlon from water solutions 1s small unless the uranium

solubility; but there 1s no well-defined relation between
the two. | |

Effect of other salts. Anions that complex uranium

 

in the agueous phase may seriously interfere wlth the ex-
traction of the latter. Chloride, fluoride, sulfate, phos-
phate, and several organlc anions have been studled for
thelr Interference. The adverse effecta of these lons may

be minimized by removing them from solution prior to uranium ex-
traction, by complexing the anlons with cations of salting-
out agents, or by using an excess of an efficient salting-
out agent to over-ride the anion interference. The inorganic
anlons may be preclpltated as sllver chloride, lanthanum
fluorlde, barium sulfate, zirconlum phosphate, or ammonium
phoaphomolybdaete. Fluoride lon 1s complexed by aluminum

and calcium. Sulfate lon 18 complexed by ferric lon. Large
amounts of sulfate :lon are also precipitated by calclum
nitrate. Phosphate lon 18 complexed by ferric and aluminum
lone. Calclum nltrate has been used to counteract the effect
of acetate and oxalate. The effect of chloride on the
partition of uranium may be reduced in the presence of a
8trong saltlng-out agent. Chloride lon 1s more objectionable
from the fact that -1t promotes the extraction of other elements,
notably lron. In the presence of large amounts of interfering
dons, particularly sulfate and phosphate, it 1s advisable to
separate the uranium from solution prior to extraction. This
may be done by preclpltation with carbonate-free ammonium
hydroxide. The preclpitate 1s dissolved in nitric acld and
the extraction 18 inltiated. Ferric hydroxide may be used

to carry trace amounts of uranium.

Uranium may be extracted from aqueous medie other than

nitrate. Thilocyanate solutions have been found satisfactory.
The extraction, however, le lees selective from thiocyanate
than from nltrate solutions.

Solvent actlon. The partition of uranyl nltrate 1s
dependent upon the free solvent concentratlon. This 1s
reflected 1n the coefficients of extractlon of micro and
macro amounts of uranium from highly salted aqueous solutions.
The partition coeffleclent of trace amounts 1s larger than
thet of large amounts as a result of more avallable solvent.
As mentioned previously, macro amounts of uranium extract
more readlly from water solutions or less highly salted
aqueous solutions than do micro amounts. This effect may
be attributed to the salting-out abllity of uranyl nitrate
itself.

The extraction of other elements 1s affected also by
the uranium concentration. High loading of the solvent
by uranium reduces the extraction of less preferred complex.
specles. High uranium loading may be achleved by diluting
the solvent with a secondary solvent in which uranyl nitrate
1s 1nsoluble or slgnificantly less soluble than in the primary
extractant. Solvent dilution, 1n general, causes a deorease

“1n the partition coefficient ,.2+3.321,320,330 Wohlhuter and
Seuteron33t have llsted a number of aromatic and chlorine-
substituted dlluents ln order of lncreasing harmfulness to
uranium extractlon: benzene, toluene, xylene, carbon tetra-
chloride and dichloroethylene, chloroform. Solvent dilution
may be used also to lmprove upon the physical properties
of the organic phase, eg., density, viscosity, etc.

The sultabllity of mixtures of oxygen-contalning
solvents as extractants for uranyl nitrate has been

.ether, diethyl ether and acetophenone, lsocamyl alcohol and
methyl 1sobutyl ketone. The enhanced extraction by solvent
mixtures has been attributed to the formatlon of mixed gol-
vates of uranyl nitrate.322:330

Re-extraction. Uranlum 1s re-extracted from the or~-

‘Extraction of other elements. A number of elements
other than uranium are extracted by oxygen-contalning 80l-
vents. Those commonly found with irradieted uranium are
hexavalent Np and Pu; pentavalent Pa; tetravalent Th, Np,
Pu, Zr(+Nb), and Ce; and ruthenium complexes. Neptunium,
plutonium, and cerium are made less extractable by reduction
to lower oxidation states. Pavorable separation of uranium
from the other elements may be achleved by control of the
nitric acid and salting-out agent concentrations. Free
halogens are extraoted. These elements may be eliminated
from solution prior to uranium extraction. The halogens
also combine chemically with a number of solvents; eg,,
lodine and hexone. The combined halogen 1s not re-extracted
by water contacts. |

} ETHERS

Diethyl ether

‘Aqueous nitrate systems. The extractlon of uranyl nitrate
by diethyl ether 1s widely used in radiochemical separations.
because of the selectivity of the extraction. Disadvantages
of the method are the high volatility and low flash point
of the solvent and the relatively low distribution of
uranium into the solvent.

The partition of uranyl nitrate between diethyl ether
and water 1s 1llustrated in figure 1.282:321 with both
. phases saturated with uranyl nitrate at 25-26°C, the dis-
tribution coefficient 1s about 0.68.328 The effect of nitric
acid upon the partition of uranium333233% ang nitric ac1a32%s
333-335 itself 1s represented in figure 8. Furman, Mundy,
and Morrison328 report that the pH of the aqueous phase
should be 4 or less for complete extraction of uranium to
occur. The influence of ammonium nitrate and calcium nitrate
upon the extraction of nitric acid 1s also shown in figure
8.328

Figurea 9 and 10 demonstrate the influence of various
salting-out agents on the distribution of uranyl nitrate.320.328
The nitrate concentratlon plotted in figure 9 includes that
of the salting-out agent plus that of uranyl nitrate left
after extraction by an equal volume of ether. The latter
contributes only a few percent to the total nitrate concen-
tration in most cases. A notable exception is the iron (IIL)
point at 1.18M nltrate concentration. In this 1lnstance,
.0.82M nitrate lon 1s attributable to ferric nitrate and
the remalnder to uranyl nitrate. The nitrate concentration
of the saltling-out agent is plotted in figure 10. Uranyl
nitrate contributes little to the nltrate concentration since
only one gram of uranyl nitrate was used per 100 grams of

inltlal aqueous solution. Furman, Mundy, and Morrison328

82
observed that the salting action of a mixture of nltrates
could be reasonably predicted by the following method:

The logarithm of a _ for each salt at a given total

nltrate molarity 18 divided by the total nitrate

molarity. These individual quotlents ere then mul-

tiplied by the nitrate molarity of the respective

salts. The sum of the resulting products 1s then

equal to the logarithm of the predicted partition

coefficlent. ,
Hellman and Wo1£336 have studled the salting action of
varlous nitrates 1n the presence of nitrlc acid and thorium
nitrate. Some of thelr results are listed in Table IX.
From the data 1t may be observed that (1) thorium nitrate
1s generally a less effectlve salting-out agent on a
normality basls than other metal nitrates and (2) the
extraction of uranlum becomes less efflclent as the amount
of extracted thorium becomes appreclable.

The effect of several forelgn anlons on the extraction
of uranyl nitrate by dlethyl ether 1s glven 1n figure 11337
and Table x.326 Arsenate, molybdate, and vandate lons also
interfere with the extraction of uranium. The effect of
these ion may be offset by the addition of ferric nitrate
to the solution.338

The partltlon of a large number of elements between
various aqueous nitrate systems and dlethyl ether 1s glven
in Table XI and illustrated 1n figures 12-14. The lnecreased
distribution of heavy elements and flssion product elements with
Increased nitric acld concentratlon should be noted. For a
selective uranium extraction the nitrle acid concentratlon
should be minimal. Hyde332 hes recommended an aqueous
phase 0.5~1M in nitric acld and 2.5M in magnesium nitrate
for the quantitative extraction of uranium by diethyl ether.
More selective extraction of uranyl nitrate may be made from
a saturated ammonium nitrate-solution, 0.05-0.1M in nitric
acid. The extractlon can be made quahtitative by repeated

contacts with ether.

Other ketonle solvents

Hyde and Wo1£318 have studled the extractlon of uranium
and thorium by methyl n-amyl ketone and dlisopropyl ketone
as a function of total nitrate concentratlon in the aqueous
phase. In both cases, uranium was better extracted than
thorium. The extraction of thorium did not become appreclable
(¢5%) until the aqueous nitrate concentration was greater
than 5M. Dilsopropyl ketone was found to be an excellent
extractant of protactinium.3L8

Vesely, Beranova, and Ma1§382 have inveastlgated the
extraction of uranium and fission products by several
methylalkyl ketones: methylhexyl, methylamyl, methlbutyl,
and methylpropyl In addition to methyl lsobutyl and methyl-
cyclohexanone. The partltlon coefficlents of both uranium
and flssion product actlvlty ‘vere measured as a function of
acid concentration in the range of -0.4 to 3M nitric acid.
In thls acldlty range, fisslon product extraction was found
to be maximum in the 0-1M nitric acld region. The partition
coefficient, Opps in this reglon was greatest wlth methyl-
propyl ketone (22 x 1073 at 0.61M) and least with methyl-
hexyl ketone (2.4 x 1073 at 0.03M). In the acid-deflcilent
reglon, Opp Increased as the acld-deficlency was decreased
(the solution was made more acidlc). After the maximum
opp was reached 1n the 0-1M acid region, the partition
coefflelent was decreased and then increased as the aqueous

solution wes made more acldlic up to 3-4M. The partition

120
coefficient of uranium, os increased as the nltric acid
concentration was lncreased over the entire range. At 3M
nltric acid, a, varied from about 0.7 for methylhexyl
ketone to about 2 for methylpropyl ketone and 3 for methyl-
cyclohexanone. The greatest separation, Pp, of uranium
from fisslon products was found In the 0.1M acid defilclent
region (-0.1M). For methylhexyl ketone B was found to be
>1500; for methylpropyl ketone P was about 400. The extraction
coefficients of uranium were <¢ 0.2 for methylhexyl ketone
and about 0.5 for methylpropyl ketone at this acid concentration.
Allen383 has tested dlisobutyl ketone, diisopropyl
ketone, and methylhexyl ketone as solvents for the purifi-
cation of uranium from iron, copper, chromium, and nickel.
Dilsobutyl ketone was found most satisfactory under the
conditions tested. Dilsopropyl extracted some iron and
chromlum. Methylhexyl ketone extracted iron, chromlum,
and copper.
Uranium and thorlum may be extracted quantitatively
from a nitrate medium by mesityl oxide.38% Under the condl-
tions tested zirconium 1s extracted to a large extent;

vanadlum and yttrium to a lesser extent; cerium only slightly.

MISCELLANEOUS SOLVENTS

Nitromethane has been recommended by. Warners22 as an
extractant for uranium. It 1s resistant to oxidation,
stable to high concentrations of ritric :acid, and highly
selective. The dlstribution of uranyl nitrate between
nitromethane and water and nitromethane and saturated ammon-
ium nitrate solution 1s given ln figure 3-B. The extraction
of thorium nitrate by nitromethane from aqueous solution is
much less than that of uranium. Color tests indicate that
neither copper, cobalt, 1lron TI), nor chromium nitrate 1s
extracted by the solvent. With dlethyl ether, considerable
amounts of copper nitrate and trace amounts of ferric nitrate
are extracted. Nitric acld enhances the extraction of uranyl
nitrate by nitromethane. However, above a critical acid
concentration (~5N Initial acid concentration with equal
phase volumes at 20°C) only one liquid phase 1s formed.

ORGANOPHOSPHORUS COMPOUNDS. Within recent years, a
large number of organophosphorous compounds have been developed
and investigated as extractants for uranium. These compounds
have been eubdivided in the present paper into neutral and

acidic organophosphorus compounds.

NEUTRAL ORGANOPHOSPHORUS COMPOUNDS

(RO)4P 0; dialkyl alkylphosphonates, (RO) RP - 0; alkyl
dialkylphosphlinates, (RO)R,P 0; and trialkylphosphlnhe oxides
RP = 0. The abllity of the solvents to extraét uranium is
in the order

This 18 also the order of increasing base strengths of the
phosphoryl oxygen .222 In Table XVII, the four types of

plutonium (IV), thorium, fission products, and acids.387

quantitatively by tributylphosphine oxide (Table XVII), other
elements are also hlghly extracted. In fact, in spite of
lower extraction coefflclents, tributyl phosphate affords a
better separation of uranium from thorium, plutonium (IV),
and fission products under the conditions listed in Table
XVII than does trihutylphosphine oxide.

The extractive cepaclty of TBP is affected considerably
by the cholce of diluent. Taube3? extracted u(vi), Np(IV),
Np(VIl), and Pu(VI) from SM HNO, aqueous solutlons with 0.15M
TBP dlssolved ln a number of solvents, including benzene and
chloroform. Larger extraction coefficlents were obtalned for
all the elements tested with benzene rather than chloroform
a8 dlluent. In the case of uranium, the difference in a, was
- greater than ten-fold. Little difference ln extractive
capaclty was observed wlth TBP diluted by benzene or carbon
tetrachloride. 8Simllar results were obtained by Dlzdar, et
21.329 Uranyl nitrate (0.0038M) was extracted from 2M nitric
acld solutions by various concentrations of TBP diluted wlth
carbon tetrachloride, xylene, kerosene, hexane, dlbutyl ether,
diethyl ether, and isopropyl ether. The partition coefflclent
was found to lnerease with increasing TBP concentration to a
maximum for pure TBP. For carbon tetrachlorlde and xylene
the maximum value was already attained at 40 mole per cent
TBP. The other dlluents are listed above ln the approximate
order 1n which they inhiblt the extractlon of uranium by TBP.
Differences in a, for various diluents, were found to become
smaller wlth increased uranium concentration. Bruce3LS has
found that the extraction of flssion products 1s also affected
by the choice of dlluent.

Tetraphenylphosphonium chloride (TPPC)
A recent study has been reported In which uranium was
extracted 1nto chloroform as the tetraphenylphosphonium uranyl

156
" tribenzoate complex. tHE Uranyl lon was converted to an anlonic

form by benzolc acid. Tetraphenylphosphonlum chloride was used
ag extractant. The extraction of uranlum was found to depend
upon pH, TPPC concentration, and uranium concentration. At
~pH 3-9, the extraction of uranlum was nearly quantitative.
The partition coefficient, ay, was increased with Increased
TPPC concentration and was decreased with increased uranium
concentration. The decrease 1n a, with increased uranium
"concentration was observed with a constant uranium-to-TPPC
molar ratio. At 25°C, and pH 5.2, zine, zirconium (niobium),
and ruthenium were appreciably extracted (~10-20% compared

to 100% for uranium). The extraction of zilno and zlrconium
may be depressed by the use of a complexing agent, EDTA, in

solution.

ls used to form the dialkylpyrophosphoric acid. A 3:1 mole
ratio should glve about equal mole quantities of mono- and
di-alkyl orthophosphorilc acids. 22 The reactions are com-
plex and mixtures of varlous phosphoric acids are formed.

Moorel8 has also Investigated the extractlon of uranium
(VI) from acetic acid solution by T1OA. Extractlons were
carried out in the same manner as those from hydrochloric
acid solution (figure 53). Aqueous solutions of varying
acetic acid concentration containing 2 x 10% alpha counts
per mlnute per ml of p233 tracer were extracted with equal
volumes of 5% (w/v) TiOA in xylene. It was found that maxi-
mum uranium extraction (>90%) is obtained from 0.5M to 1M
acetic acid solutions. The addition of 3%(v/v) butyl cello-
solve to the Ti10A-xylene solution inhibits foaming during
the extraction process. By lncreasing the T10A concentration,
macro amounts of uranium are efflclently extracted. Greater
than 95% stripping may be achleved by contacting the amine-
Xylene phase with an equal volume of 0.5M HNO, 3M H,S0),
6M H,S0),, 1M NH, HCO, concentrated NH),OH, or 0.25M HF-0.25M
HNO4 solution. From 0.5M-1M aceblc acid solutlon, ruthenium
(11.5%), zirconium (27.9%), and niobium (11.1%) are extracted.
Separation 1s made from strontium (alkaline earths), ceslum
and europium (rare earths), plutonium (III) (trivalent actin-
ides), thorium, protactinium, hafnium, tantalum, lron, lead,
nickel, cobalt, manganese, chranium (111), aluminum, copper,
zinc, blsmuth, tin,and antimony. F112 476 The selectlvity may
be improved if the uranium is first precipitated by hydroxide,
dissolved with 1M acetic acld, and then extracted as pre-
viously described. Iron hydroxide 1s used to carry trace

amounts of uranium in the precipitation step. }

carbonate was found the most effliclent stripping agent on a
counter-current basls.

The extraction of uranlum by acetylacetone-chloroform
in the presence of sodium chloride and EDTA has been
studled by Tabushi . 120 Sodium chloride increases the ex-
traction yield and broadens the favorable pH range. EDTA

Ammonium salt N-nitrosophehylhydroxylamine (cupferron),

170
Lhe, NH}.

Cupferron 1s an important reagent in the analytical
separation of uranium. The reagent precipitates uranium
(IV) from acidic (H,80, or HCl) solution but not uranium
(VI). By converting uranium to lta two oxidation states,
separatlon can be made alternatlvely from elements not pre-
cipitated by cupferron and from those preclpltated by the
reagent, The uranium (IV) cupferrate complex, U(Cup),
was found by Auger2i2 to be soluble in chloroform and neutral
organic solvents. Furman, et 21.220 found milligram amounts
of uranium (IV) to be incompletely extracted from agueous
acld solution by hydrogen cupferrate ln chloroform but to

be almost completely extracted by ethereal hydrogen cup-

197
ferrate; l.e., cupferron extracted by ether from an acid
solution. Ethereal hydrogen cupferrate was also found to
extract quantitatively macroamounts of uranium {IV) from
(1 + 19) sulfuric acid containing hydroxylamine hydrochloride
and submilligram amounts from (1 + 19) sulfuric acid in the
presence of saturated mercury-zinc amalgem.229 The partition
coefficient, %(Iv)’ 18 incressed wlth Increased cupferrate
concentration and 1s decreased wlth increased acid concen-
tration.222

A uranium (VI) cupferrate complex 1s precipitated by
the reagent from neutral solutions. There appear to be two
forms, one of which 1s soluble in ethyl ether,220 From
(1 + 9) sulfuric acid, milligram amounts of uranium (VI)
are extracted by an equal volume of chloroform with an ex-
cess of eupferron present .22°

The extraction of uranium (VI) cupferrate from aqueous.
perchlorate solution by hexone and chloroform is given in
figure 62 as a function of the pH of the final aqueous
solution.23 Chloroform 1s a poor solvent for the complex.
Hexone 1s better, but quantitative extraction 1s not achieved
by a single contact of the solvent with an equal volume of
the aqueous solution. The extraction curves for h,298 Sm,
and 12299 are also glven in the figure.

The properties of other metal cupferrates have been
reviewed by Furman, Mason, and Pekola.222

formed only wlth high concentrations of uranium and reagent.
The uranyl-DDTC complex 1s practlcally inextractable by
carbon tetrachloride but is readily extracted by isoamyl
alcohol, dlethyl ether, and amyl acetate .223 Others have
_ used hexone , 222 ethyl acetate,222 chloroform,228 and ben-
zene22L to extract the complex. Employing the above sol-
vents, the U(VI)-DDTC complex has been extracted from
aqueous solutions having a wlde range of pH, e.g., pH 1-522L
and pH 6.5-8.3.222 Sodlum tartrate has been used to prevent
hydrolysis at higher pH values .222 The U(VI)-DDTC complex
1s extracted in the presence of EDTA. Uranium may then be
Separated from elements such as thorium,that form strong EDTA
complexes 22+: 527,528 Uranium may be further separated from
those elements extracted as DDTC complexes by stripping the
former 1nto an ammonlum carbonate solution.126:525

Anion exchange. Anion exchange resins commonly used
in the radlochemical laboratory are the strong base resins
such as Dowex-l and-2 and Amberlite IRA-410 and IRA-400.
The capaclty of these resins 1s approximately 2.5 milli-
equlvalents per gram of resin. Weak base resins are also

avallable. However, thelr use is more limited. These

resins have capacities ranging from about 6 to 10 mllli-
equivalents per gram of resin.

Splvey, et 21.23% have lnvestlgated varlous factors
such as resin capaclty, resin phase volume anion adsorption,
etc, that affect the sorption of uranium. Trivisonno232
has made a literature survey of factors that influence the
adsorption and elution of uranium by and from strong base
anion exchange resins. These are slmllar to the factors
influencing solvent extractlon and include, other than
those already mentloned, uranium concentration, anien con-
centration, pH, the presence of other metallic lons and
foreign anions, temperature, resin size, porosity, cross-
linkage, ete.

The varlous systems from which uranium may be adsorbed
by anlon exchange resins are described below. The resin
may be converted to a particular anionic form by washing
wlth an approprlate solution.

Numerous separations of uranlum from other elements
are poselble using hydrochleric acid systems. The more ob-
vious ones are those in which uranium 1s adsorbed and the
other element is not. Consideration of figure 65, indicates
that uranium can be separated from alkali metals, alkaline
earths, aluminum, yttrium, rare earths, actinium, and thorium
by adsorption as uranium (VI) on a strong base anlon exe
change resin from a concentrated hydrochloric acid solution.
Trivalent actinide elements are not adsorbed from hydro-
chloric acid sclutions. Plutonium is eluted as Pu (III) with
12M HCl containing hydroxylamine hydrochloride and NH), I,
NH, I alone, or HI. Separatlons may be made by adsorption of
the contaminating element and elution of uranium with dilute
hydrochloric acid. For exsmple, molybdenum 1s adsorbed from
0.1M He1.243 Bismuth is also adsorbed from dilute (<1M)
ney. 24.545 Other elements that show strong adsorption from
dilute HCl include many of the transition metals, tin,
tellurlum, and polonium.238 Kraus and Moorea have effected
the separation of protactinlum and uranium by adsorbing them
from 8M HCl on a column of Dowex A-1 resin and developing
the column wlth 3.8M HCl. Protactinlum appeared first in
the eluent, separated from uranium. The uranium fraction
contalned; however, a falr amount of protactinium "talling'.

Advantage may be taken of the different distribution
coefficients exhlblted by ions 1n varlous oxidatlon states
to effect thelr separatlon from uranium. Iron reduced to
ferrous lon by hydrogen 10aideL or ascorbic acia28 1s
separated by elution with 4M HCl. U(IV) may be separated
from Pa(IV) and Th(IV). U(IV) 1s adsorbed by Amberlite
IRA-401 (100 mesh) and Dowex-1 (100-200 mesh) from >8M HCl.

Nelther Pa(IV) nor Th(IV) 1s adsorbed from 6M - 12.6M He1.249
The elution of Pu(III) by 12M HCl from strong base anion
exchange resln has already been mentloned. Wish and Rowe1122%
have effected the separation of Th, Pu, Zr, and Np from U
by elutlon with hydrochloric acld in a sequence of concen-

wlth 7.5M HCl; neptunlum (IV) with a 6M HCl - 5% NH, OH * HC1
solution. Uranium is finally eluted with 0.1N HCl.

Korkisch, ot a1,22L have separated uranlum from tung-
sten by means of anion exchange. The uranium is adsorbed
on Dowex -1 resin from a solution containing 20% 4M HCY and
80% ethanol (volume #). Ascorbic acid 1s used to reduce
any lron present. The resin ls washed with a similar solu-
tion and uranium 1s eluted with an ether-saturated 0.1M HCl

solution. No tungsten 1s observed in the final eluate.

Fluoride systems.
Paris222 has reported the adsorptlon of elements from

hydrofluoric acid solutions with Dowex-1l anion exchange
resin (x10, 200 mesh). His results are shown in figure

68. Uranium (VI) adsorption is strong from dilute HF
solutions and decreases with lnoreased acld concentratlon.
Separation from elements exhiblting no or strong adsorption
from HF solutions may be achleved by proper selectlon of the
aold concentration. Elements such as Be, B, Se¢, Ti, Zr, Mo,
Sn, Te, Hf, Ta, W, Re, and Hg have adsorption curves similar
in shape to that of uranium (VI). Separation from these
elements using an HF system should prove difficult to almost
impossible, depending upon the distribution coefficlents in-

volved.

Carbonate systems.’

Uranium 18 recovered from carbonate leach llquors by anlon
exchange ln industrial operations. Its anlon exchange behavior
1s similar in carbonate solution to that in sulfate solution.
That is, the distribution coefflclent 1s decreased wlth lncreased
carbonate concentration. Thls ia illustrated in flgure 72 for
ammonium carbonate solutions.2[2 A plmllar decrease In D 1s
observed for increased sodium carbonate concentration.2%8 The
distribution coefficlent 1s also decreased by a decrease in pH
of the solution. The increase ln bicarbonate concentration at
the lower pH interferes with uranium adsorption. Other anions
such as sulfate; nitrate, and chlorlde may also lnterfere with
uranium adsorption from carbonate solution. To prevent gassing
with carbon dloxide, uranium 1s eluted with salt solutiors rather
than acids.

Vanadium and phosphate and molybdate2(2 have been separated
from uranium in carbonate solutions by anion exchange. The im-
purities are adsorbed on the resin together with uranium and
eluted with a 10% Na,CO4 solution. Uranium is eluted with a 5%
NaCl solution.

has been used by w1sn280 who loaded a column of Dowex-2 resin

was eluted with 0.1M HCl - 0.06M HF. An alternative method

was eluted with 1.0N H3POy after washing the column with an

an alcoholic HCl gas solution. Uranium was eluted with a 0.1M
HCl - 0.06M HF solution and molybdenum with 12M HNO,.

5. Volatllizatlon. Uranium may be separated from many elements by
fractional distillation of the volatlle compound, uranium hexa-
fluoride. This method of separation has been applied to the
recovery of uranlum from lrradiated fuel elements. Katz and
Rabinowitz: have reviewed many of the early methods for the pre-
paration of UFg: fluorinatlon of various uranium compounds with
elemental fluorine or cobalt trifluoride, disproportionation of
UF whlch results in both UF) and UF, and the reaction between
UF, and dry oxygen which results in UO, F, and UFg. The latter
two methods are not very practical from an analytical standpoint.
Other fluorlnating agents that form UFg Include ceric fluoride,
manganic fluoride, silver difluoride, halogen fluorides (e.g.,

BrF., and C1F;) and fused metalllec fluorides. Most elements

3
form fluorides under the condltions that UFg 1s obtalned. How-
ever, only a small number of these fluorides are volatile. Hyman,
et 21.3 have published a table of some 26 elements having fluorides
with boiling or sublimation points of 550°C or less. Included in
this group are the fluorides of boron, sllicon, phosphorus,
vanadium, sulfur, tungsten, blsmuth, plutonlum, and the fisslon
products, germanium, arsenlec; selenium, niobium, molybdenum,
ruthenium, antimony, tellurium, and lodine. The boiling point

of UF 1s 54.6°C.. Non-volatile fluorides from which uranium is
readlly separated include those of the alkali metals, alkaline
earths, rare earths, Fe, Co, Nl, Ag, Al, B¢, Mn, Tl, Pb, Zn, Cu,
Hg, Cd, and zr 34.622

Uranium does not form & volatlle compound by interactlon

wlth anhydrous hydrogen fluoride. Materials such as Nb, Ta, As,

231
Sb, S51, Te, Se, etc. do. 3% The oxides of titanium, tungsten,
and molybdenum react slowly wlth HF. Vo05 and VN also react
slowly. VO, Vo0y and Vo0q are not volatilized. 3% Rodden and
Ware desoribe a procedure that makee use of both anhydrous
hydrogen fluoride and fluorine in the separation of uranium.
Possible contaminants of the separated UFg lnclude Cr, Ta, W, Mo,
or V.

Uranium hexachloride and uranium (IV) borohydride are
volatile compounds for which procedures mlght be developed for
the separation of uranium.

Uranium may be separated from arsenic, antimony, blsmuth,
selenium, and tin by volatilization of the latter elements with
a mixture of hydrobromlc acld and bromine. 138

6. Electrochemical methods. The electrolysis of dilute sulfuric acld
solutions with a mercury cathode results in the quantitative
deposition of Cr, Fe, Co, Nl, Cu, Zn, Ga, Ge, Mo, Rh, Pd, Ag, Cd,
In, Sn, Re, Ir, Pt, Au, Hg, and Tl in the cathode.2%3 Arsenio,
selenium, tellurium, osmium, and lead are quantitatively separated
from the electrolyte, but are not quantitatively reposited 1n
the cathode. 213 Manganese, ruthenium, and antimony are incom-
pletely separated.2:3 Uranium and the remalnling actinlde elements,
rare earth elements, the alkali and alkaline eerth metals,
aluminum, vanadium, zirconium, nlobium, etc. remain in solu-
tion.223 casto923 and Rodden and Ware3% have reviewed the effects
of many variables in the electrolytic separation of the above=~
named elements from uranium. According to Rodden and Warf,3%
optimum conditions for the purification of uranium in sulfuric
acld solutions wlth a mercury cathode are: electrolyte volume,

50 ml; free sulfuric acld concentration, 1N; current density,
as high ae practicable with the given acid concentration (about
10 amp maximum); anode, flat platinum spiral or grid just

making contact wlth the surface of the electrolyte; cathode area,

232
a8 large as practicable; stirring, surface of the mercury cathode
is stirred rather rapldly; temperature of electrolyte, between
25° and 40°C; mercury for the cathode, pure; anions, chloride,
nitrate, and phosphate lons should be absent, or present in
only small amounts.

Uranium may be deposlted electrolytically at the cathode
of a cell from acetate, 024-631 carbonate, 832 oxalate, 933-638
formate, 232 phosphate ,240 fluoride ,232:641 and chloride242
solutions. * Many of the uranlum electrodepositlon procedures
have been developed in an effort to prepare thin, uniform films
for alpha and fission counting rather than to separate the ele-
ment from any particular impurity. However, in the work of
Smith and co-workergo2t, 626 and Coomans®2L urahlum was separated
from alkall and alkaline earth metals and zlnc. Casto®23 and
Rodden and Ware3t review much of the material pertinent to the

electrodeposition of uranium.

In acid systems (cells 1, 2, 3, and 4), the transport of sulfate,
nitrate, and chloride ions to the anode results 1ln a removal of
acid and a subsequent increase in pH in the cathode compartment.
The uranium, reduced during electrolysis, 1s precipitated as

Uo, or U(HPOY),- In the alkaline system, the transport of so-

2
dium lone 18 also accompanied by a rise in pH in the cathode
compartment and uranlum is again preclpltated as the dioxide or
as a mixture of dloxide and sodium polyuranate.

The electrodilalytic separation of uranium from metals in
a complex mixture has been demonstrated by Willard and Finley.S%%
An ammonium bicarbonate solution contalning U, Fe, Ni, Cu, Cr,
Zn, Al, Mo, Mg, and Na salts, and traces of other elements was
electrolyzed in a two-compartment cell having a cation permeable
membrane and a mercury cathode. The solution was firet made
the catholyte (electrolyte in the cathode compartment) and elec-
trolyzed. Iron (80%), nickel and copper (95%), tin, and zinc
were removed from solution by deposition. The blcarbonate solu-
tion was then made the anolyte and electrolyzed at a platinum
anode. All aluminum, molybdenum, ammonium, and silicon, and some
sodium and megnesium were separated from the uranium by migra-
tlon. Uranium was retained as the carbonate complex and was
recovered as the oxlde by evaporation of the anolyte.

Other features of the electrodlalytic behavior of uranium
that may be useful in its separation and purification are (1)

the retention of uranium during electrodialysis from a perchloric

234
catholyte using an anion selective membrane, (2) the dissolution

and separation of lmpure UF)» and (3) the feasibility of .electro-

dlalysis in organic solutions. 2%

Te Pyrometallurgical processes. Although pyrometallurgical or high
temperature processes have been designed primarily for large
scale recovery of fertile material from irradiated fuel elements,
some of the methods may find application In the radlochemistry
laboratory. Types of pyrometaliurgleal operations that have
received considerable attention are(l) distillation, (2.) salt

extraction, (3.) molten metal extraction, (4 ) oxidative slagging,

(5 )} electro-refining, and{6 ) decomposition of uranium iodide.

These methods have been reviewed by Lawroski 211

1. Plutonium 1s concentrated by vacuum dlstillation
from molten uranium at 1500-1800°C.

2. Plutonium ls extracted from molten uranlum by salts
such as UF, or MgCl,. Uranium remalns in the metallic
state. Pidtonium 18 recovered as a halide salt.

3. Plutonium 1s extracted by molten metals, such as
sllver or magnesium, that are immiscible with molten
uranium. Flsslon products are also extracted.

4, oxidative slagging involves the preferential formation
of the most stable oxldes by a molten irradlated fuel
element in a limlted oxygen environment. These oxides
(rare earths) float to the surface of the molten ma-
terlal and are skimmed off. Other oxides diffuse into
the erucible and through the slag layer.

5. In electro-refining, uranium is dissolved anodically
in a fused salt bath of alkall or alkaline earth halides
that contaln a uranium compound. Noble metals do not
dissolve and are deposited as anode sludge. Uranium
and chemically similar materlals are deposited at the
cathode. Alkall, alkaline earth, and rare earth fission
products concentrate in the salt bath..

6. Uranium 1s recovered as the metal from the thermal de-
composition of UI. Zirconium and nlobium are the principal
contamlnants.

" It is not the purpose of this section to describe the
technlques involved in pyrometallurglcal processes. The interested
reader may consult the many papers presented in "Progress in
Nuclear Energy, Series III, Process Chemistry,” volumes 1(1956)

and 2(1958), and in the "Proceedings of the International Con-

235
ference on Peaceful Uses of Atomlc Energy,” volumes 9(1956) and

17(1958).
IV-E Determination of Urarilum

The amount of uranium ln a sample may be determined by
standard methods of analysis: gravimetric, volumetrie, colori-
metric, spectrophotometric, ete. 34a 191-195,197,198,200,645
‘Because of 1ts natural radioactivity, uranlum may also be deter=
mined by counting techniques. The appllcablility, in terms of
mass range, of various methods for the determination of uranium
1s glven in Table oovrI. A222
1. Counting techniques. Principles of alpha, beta, and gamma

counting are consldered ln revlew articles by Steinberg,

Hanna, S41 Deutsch and Kofold-Hansen, 248 Crouthame1, 242 and
Jarfey. 220 All three methods of counting are applicable to

the radiometric determination of uranium since both alpha- and
beta-emitting isotopes exist (Section III). Spontaneous fission
half=11ives have been determined for several uranium lsotopes:
v3, u?34, g235, 7236, 7238, These lsotopes are too long-lived,
however, to make fisslon counting a practical method for thelr
determination.

Ionization chambers are most commonly used for the detection
of alpha particles. In flgures 77 and 78 are shown the alphas
spectra of y235 and y233, respectively. The spectra were obtalned
with a parallel plate, Frisch grid lonlzation chamber using P-10
(90% argon, 10% methane) gas. A multi-channel analyzer was used
in conjunction with the ionization chamber. Both y235 and y233
samples were prepared by volatilization. Flgure 79 represents

Samples of the metastable y235 1somer have been prepared
by electrostatically collecting the recoll atoms of Pu239 in
air. 951.658 A negative potential of several hundred volts was
applied to the metalllc collection plate.

Carswell and Milstea®d2 have succeeded in preparing thin
sources by a spraylng technique. The material to be deposited
1s dissolved as the nitrate in an organic solvent (alcohol or

acetone). The solution 1s drawn into a fine glass capillary

246
tube and sprayed onto the backing materilal by applylng an
electric field.

Electrodeposition 1s a generally satisfactory method for
preparing uniform samples wlth quantitative or nearly quantita-

tive ylelds. Uranium has been plated from a varlety of solu-
tlons: acetate, 34s 623,628-631 formate, 232 oxalate,o2 633-638

carbonate, 2 632 fluoride, 2 632,641 and chloride .S42 A satis-

factory electrolyte for the depopitlon of uranium is 0.4M
ammonium oxalate. 25% A rotating platinum anode 18 used to stir
the solutlon placed ln a vertical cylindrical cell. The cell

1s made of glass, luclte, or some other chemlcally inert material.
The cathode on whlch the uranium 1s to be depasited is thoroughly
cleansed and made the bottom of the cell. The assembled cell

1s placed 1ln a hot water bath and the temperature kept at about
80°C. A current denslty of approximately 0.1 amp/cm® 1s used.
The deposition 1s influenced strongly by the rate of stirring,
current density, and presence of foreign tons.25%

Vacuum sublimation provides an excellent means for the
preparation of thin depoeslts of uranium. The sublimation of
uranium acetylacetonate, U(CgH;0,) ys has been used. A more
convenlent method 1s the sublimation of uranium oxides. A
uranium salt solutlon ls placed on a tungsten or tantalum ribbon
supported between two electrodes. The solution 1s dried by a
heat lamp or by passing a low current through the metal ribbon.
The sample backing material 1s suspended at a sultable height
above the metal ribbon. A bell jar 1s placed over the assembled
unit and evacuated. The uranium ls volatilized by increasing
the current through the metal ribbon. The uniformity of the
deposit depends upon the distance between the ribbon and the
backing plate. The collection efficlency also depends upon
this dlstance but in an inverse manner to that of deposit uni=
formity. Usually a compromise ig made between collection

<°
efficiency and sample uniformity. Much of the uranium that is

247
not collected on the sample plate can be recovered from masking
plates and glass chimneys placed between the filament and backing
material. The collection effilclency may also be improved by
subliming from furnaces so that the beam of uranium molecules

1s directed toward the backing plate. . The furnace 1s heated

‘by electron bombardment or induction heating.

3. Activation Analysis. In activation analysis, a nucllde
irradiated by neutrons, gamma rays, or charged particles 1s
transformed Into a radloactlve nuclide more easily detected than
the original one. The amount of original materlal may be deter=
mined either absolutely or comparatively. For an absolute deter-
mination, the cross section of the reaction, the irradiation flux,
and the disintegration rate of the reaction product must be known
or determined. For comparative analysls, a substance of unknown
‘mass is irradiated simultaneously with a glmilar substance of
known mass. The positions of these two substances are elther
side by elde or, if separated, ln positions of like flux. The
reactlon product activities of the two samples are compared to
glve the relative masses of the startling materials. The com-
parative technique 1s, 1n general, much easler to apply. The
uncertalnties of many variables are eliminated by relative
measurements.

Activation with thermal neutrons may be successfully
employed as a method of analysis for--natural uranium, uranium-
236, and the fissionable isotopes of uranium. Natural uranium
consists of 1238(99.3%), u235(0.72%), and v23*(0.0057%) . £51 The

principal reactiorsof these nuclides with thermal neutrons are:

Other than e352, y233 is the best uranium isotope to determine

Excitation functions have been determined for a number of
reactions with charged partlcles or gamma rsys incident on
uranlum lsotopes. These reactlons may be used for activation
analysls. For absolute analysis, it should be pointed out that
(1) the cross sections reported are sometimes subject to con-
slderable error; (2) energy determinations of the incoming
particle or ray are also subject to error; and (3) the reaction
product can often be produced by a number of reactions. Com-
parative analysls appears to be a much better method for the
determination of uranium. For gamma-ray (bremsstrahlung) activation,
slmultaneous irradiations in a like flux are falrly easy to
accomplish. The two samples; unknown and standard, are mechani-
cally rotated in the gamma-ray beam. For charged particle
activation, the simultaneous irradiation of twd samples in a
like flux may require some ingenuity on the part of the ex-
perimenter.

IV-F. Dissolution of Uranium Samples

1. Metallle uranium.

HNO. Uranium metal dissolves in nitric acid to form uranyl
nitrate. With massive amounts of uranlum the rate of dissolu-
tion 1s moderately rapia.2lL The reaction between uranium
turnings, powder, or sintered metal and nitric acid vapors or
nitrogen dloxlde may occur with explosive violence .2lT Oxldes

of nltrogen are the principal gaseous products in the dlssolution

of the metal by HNO The presence of oxygen ln the dissolver

system tends to reduce the emlssion of these oxides. 88 The
rate of dissolution of large amounts of metallic uranium may

be increased by the addition of small amounts of sulfuric, 2
phosphoric, 212.680 or perchlor1ct8L acld to the nitrile acld.
H,80,. Hot concentrated sulfurle acid attacks uranium metal
slowly forming uranium (IV) sulfate lL Sulfuric acid-hydrogen

peroxide mixtures react slowly with the metal at 75°C forming

uranyl sulfate.282 The addltion of small amounts of chlorlde
or fluoride to H,S0), - H,0, mixtures increases the dissolution
rate .282

H3POy,. Cold 85% phosphoric acld attacks uranium metal slowly. oT
Concentration of the acld by heating produces a falrly rapld
reaction in which uranium (IV) phosphate 1s formed. If heated
too long, a chemically ilnert, glassy substance 1s formed.

HC10,,. Uranium metal 1s Inert toward cold, dilute perchloric acid.

 

As the concentration 1s increased by heating a point 1s reached
at which the reactlon proceeds with violence.2l12682 Oxldlzling
agents added to dilute perchloric acld dissolve the meta1.8L
HCl. Concentrated hydrochloric acld vigorously attacks uranium
metal. Dilution of the acld diminishes the attack. But even

with 4M HCl there 1s a rapid evolution of hydrogen 282 A

252
finely dlvided, black preclpitate soon forma after dissolution
begins. This preclpltate 1s not dissolved by heating. Only by
the addition of oxidizing agents (hydrogen peroxide, bromine,
chlorate, nltrate, persulfate, dichromate, or ferric ions) does
the precipitate dissolve. Gaseoup chlorine, aldéd by small
amounte of iror or iodine, also oxldlizes and solublllzes the
uranlum preclplitate. The addition of small amounts of fluosilicle
acl 682,68 or large smounts of phosphoric ac1a882 to the hydro-
chlorlc acid prevents formation of the black precipitate during
the dissolution of uranium metal.

HF. The reactlon of hydrofluoric acid with uranium metal ls slow
even at temperatures of 80°-go°c.8IL The reaction 1s inhibited
by the formation of insoluble UF), on the surface of the metal.
HBr. Hydrobromie acid attacks metallic uranium in manner

similar to, but slower than, hydrochloric acta. The black
precipitate is formed.

HI. The reaction between uranlum metal and hydriodic acid is
slow. LL

Organic aclds. Acetic, formic, proplonlc, and butyric aclds

4, Meteorites, minerals, and ores. The extraction of uranium
from natural deposits may be accomplished by decomposition and
dissolution of the entire sample lnecluding uranium or by leach-
ing the uranium from the sample. Grinding and roasting facilli-
tate the recovery. Roasting removes organic material. It also
helps form soluble uranlum compounds.

Decomposition of the sample may be accomplished by acld
attack, by fuslon,or by a comblnatlon of the two. Mineral acids,
individually or in comblnatlion, may be used. The presence of
hydrofluoric acld generally alds in dlssolution. Ores, sand,
ete. may be fused with sodium carbonate, sodium hydroxide,
sodtum peroxide, sodlum bisulfate, sodium chloride and sodium
hydroxide, ammonium sulfate, potassium bifluoride, and mage
neslum oxide. Ls34,604 The melt 1s solubilized in water or acid
and the separation of uranium made by procedures outlined in
Section IV-D. Rodden and Ware3t have descrlbed a number of
procedures in which uranium was made soluble by acld attack or
by fusion methods. The recovery of uranium from monazite sands
has been reported by Calklns, et 21.59%

Acid and alkaline leaching ae used on an industrlal scale

" for the recovery of uranium frem its ores. In acld leaching,

255
hydrochloric, nitric, or sulfuric acld may be used. Indus~
trially, sulfuric acld is used because of its economy. Oxidizing
agents (Fe(III), MnO,, eta.) are used to convert uranium (IV) to
uranium (VI). A separation of uranium and thorium with an oxalic
acld~-nitric acld leach solution has been reported.S22

In alkaline leaching, various combinations of alkaline
carbonates, hydroxides, and peroxides have been used.: Indus-
trially, uranium is dissolved by alkaline carbonates as the
70, (C04) 3" complex. - Oxygen or other sultable oxidants are used:
to oonvert urenium (IV) to uranium (VI). Hydroxyl ions are
formed by the dissolution of uranium in carbonate solutions. The
presence of blcarbonate leon in the dissolving solution prevents
the precipitation of uranium. The recovery of uranium by acid
and alkaline leaching 1s reviewed in General Review reference
13 (Section I).
5. Blologlcal samples. The determination of uranium in blolo-
gical samples 18 reviewed by Steadman.298 Uranium may be ex=
tracted and determined directly from 1iquld samples. The sample
may also be ashed, as are solid samples, prior to urenium ex-
traction. Ashing nay be carried cut as a wet or dry process.
Wet-ashing 1s commonly done wlth a nitric acld solution. Ashlng
may be completed with perchloric acld. However, extreme caution
must be exerclsed when heating organic materials with perchlorlc
acld. The ashed residue 1s dissolved in aold and the uranium
determination continued from there. Wet-ashing need not be
‘carrled to completion. Analysis may be made upon the sample
after 1t has been thoroughly digested in acid.
6. Alr dust samples. Samples of air dust are commonly collected
on filter papers. The uranium may be dissolved by digesting
the sample in nitric acld solution or the sample may be ashed

and the residue dissolved in aoeild.

V. Collection of Detalled
Procedures

A procedure for the determination of uranium may entail
one or more purification steps as outlined in the preceding
sections. For example, uranium may be separated from impuriltiles
by a serles of solvent extractions with one or more different
solvents. These may be interspersed wlth precipitation and/or
lon exchange methods. The procedures described herein have
been gathered from projeet reports, the open literature, and
by private communication. Only a limlted number are presented.
They have been selected because they represent many of the
separation methode already described or because they represent
different problems in handling samples: problems of dissolution,
extraction in the presence of hlgh beta-gamma activity, etc.
A number of the procedures described do not make use of the ra-
diometric determination of uranlum. The method of separation
in these procedures, however, is applicable to radlochemlcal
“analysis and is, therefore; lncluded. A number of papers and
reports describe, in detall, procedures for the determination
of uranium. Thése should be noted. The work of Rodden and
wart has frequently been mentloned in this paper. In addl-
tion to procedures for the precipitatlon, solvent extraction,
volatilization, and electrodeposltion of uranium, these authors
have presented a number of selected procedures for the solution
of ores and minerals and the separation and determination of
uranium. Procedures for the analytical determination in naturally
occurring materials have algo been described by Rodden and
Tregonning, 38 Grimaldl, May, Fletcher, and T1tcomb,29 Sochoeller
and Powell, 293 and in the "Handbook of Chemical Determination

of Uranium in Minerals and ores. "898

The recent publication by
Mooret[L on extraction with amines contalns a collection of pro-

cedures, many of which have to do with the separation of uranium.

ROCEDURE 1: Uranlium-237.

Source: B. Warren, LA-1567 (1953) p. 18.

Editor's note: Uranium-237 may be separated from fission pro-
ducts, neptunlum, and plutonium more easlly by lon exchange and/or
solvent extraction techniques (see, for example, Procedure 7).

The following procedure 1s, however, an excellent example of

1. Introduction

The significant steps 1n the determination of u237 in ma-
terials contalning fission products, neptunium and plutonium are
the following. Rare-earth, neptunium, and plutonium activities
are removed by appropriate lanthanum fluoride scavenglng steps
in the presence of hydroxylamine hydrochloride. The latter
reagent serves to reduce both neptunium and plutonium so that
they may be carried down, and also to complex uranium and pre=-
vent 1lts later removal 1n lron scavenglng steps. Barlum and
zlrconlum are preolpltated by barlum fluozlirconate scavenglng.
followlng a cycle of ferric hydroxide scavenging and ammonium
dluranate preclpltation steps, uranium 1s reduced by zlnc metal
in hydrochloric acld medlum and precilpltated, presumably as
U(OH),, with ammonlum hydroxide. The uranium 1s further purifled
by alternate conversions to tetrafluoride and hydroxide. 24.14
23% (ux, ) which has grown in from 238 is removed by a zirconium
lodate scavenge and the uranium 1s converted to ammonium dluranate.
Uranium is finally plated from nitric acid medium onto a platinum
foll. “After flaming of the foll and welghling, uranium 1s beta
counted as Us0g- Chemical ylelds average 50 to 65%. Quadrupli-

cate determinations require approximately 8 hours.

PROCEDURE 2: Purification of Uranlum-240.

Source: E. K. Hyde and M. H. Studler, ANL-4182 (1948).
Egjtor's note: The followlng procedure was used bo BYELTY

u formed by the second order neutron capture of . The
principal decontaminating step 18 the ether extraction of
uranium from a reducing aqueous solution. Uranium 1s further

purified by a number of precipltations that are not described
in detall. These, however, are falrly easy to perform.

Irradlatlon and Chemlecal Procedure

Two grams of depleted uranium (1 part ge35 per 30,000 parts
238) as U30g in a small 2g aluminum capsule was 1rradiated in
the Hanford plle for 12 houre including time for startup and
shutdovm. S1x hours after the end of the irradiation the cap-
pule and its contents were dissolved in nltrlc acld, using
mercuric ion as catalyst for dlssolving the aluminum. The
uranlum was extracted batchwiee, the dlassolved aluminum serving
as a salting agent. The ether contalning the uranlum was then
passed through two static wash columns packed with 3/32 inch
stalnless steel helices and filled with a solution 10M in
ammonium nitrate, 0.1 M in nitric acld, 0.01 N in ferrous lon
and 0.1 M in urea. Neptunlum was reduced by the aluminum in
the dissolver and by the ferrous lon in the wash columnmg to an
unextractable oxldation state (Np IV and Np V). Additional
ether was passed through the columns to strip out the uranium.
These operations were carrled out by remote control behind
lead shielding. The Inltlal dissolver solution measured roughly
50 roentgens per hour at 8 inches. The ether solution emerging
from the second column and containing the uranium measured only
about 3 mr per hour at the surface, and most of thls was ether-
soluble iodine fission product activity. The uranium was ex-
tracted from the ether into an aqueous ammonium. sulfate solu-
tion and washed several times with ether to remove lodlne
actlvlity. LaFq was preclpltated from the uranyl nitrate solu-
tion after reduction with sulfur dloxide to remove any traces

of Np=32 which might have come through the ether extractlon.

265
PROCEDURE 2 (Continued)

»

The uranium was further purified by preolpltation as diuranate,
sodium uranyl acetate, and peroxide and by a final ether ex-
traotion. Throughout this final serles of puriflcatlons there
was no detectable decrease in p-activity; this indicates that
the uranium was radloactlvely pure.

Small aliquots of the final uranium solution were evaporated
on platinum discs and ignited to U30g to study changes 1n
activity. The remaining uranium solution was used for extraction

of neptunium daughter fractlons.

PROCEDURE 3: Purification of Irradiated U23°,

Source: S. Fried and H. Selig, Private communication.

Editor's note: The present procedure was used 1n an experiment
designed to measure the hs mal neutron fission cross section

of The amount of that can be tolerated 1n such an
experiment 1s very emall.

Two criteria were used in selectlng the purification steps

in the followlng procedure:

1) To obtaln uranium free of fission products and other
extraneous activitles wlthout Introducing contamlnant
normal uranium ln the procedure.

2) The initial part should lend itself easlly to remote

control manipulation.

"The reagenta used were carefully purified. Thus, the niltrie
acld and perchloric acid were redistilled in a2 quartz still.

The NH, NO was prepared from gaseous ammonia and distilled HNO.
The HCl was prepared by passing HCl gas into triply distilled

H,0, ete.

PROCEDURE 3 (Continued)

Procedure:
A. In Cave
The irradiated uranium oxide (~0.3 mg) was dissolved in
concentrated HNO, and made up to 2 M in HNO; with distilled H,0
to glve total volume of about 15 ml. Some Fett was added to
keep Pu and Np in +4 state. The solutlon was saturated with
NH) NO and contacted four times with 10 ml portions of ether.
Each contact was scrubbed twice with 2 M HNO saturated with
NH) NO3. The comblned ether extracts were back extracted three
times wlth 5 ml portions of H,0. The 'H,;0 strip was evaporated to
dryness and treated wlth HCl to destroy NH) NO, carried over.
B. Outside Cave

The sample could now be handled easlly outelde the cave
with a minimum ‘of shielding, most of the activity being due to
v237, A mass spectrometrlc analysls showed 1t contalned 0.5
welght % of U23!. A fission count showed that additional puri-
238

ficatlon was necessary to remove Np formed by (n,y) on Np2 37
which had bullt up during lrradlatlon.

The sample was taken up ln about 0.5 ml 6 N HCl and put
on a small Dowex-1l column and washed. The Np comeg off 1n
6 N HCl. Finally the uranium was eluted with 0.5 M HCl. The
eluate was evaporated to dryness and taken up ln 0.2 ml of 5 M
HCl, O.1 M KI and 0.05 M NH, OH o 2HCl. This was heated at 90°
for 2 minutes, diluted to 0.5 M in HCl and TTA extracted twice
for 15 minutes. The original fraction was washed twice with
benzene and evaporated to dryness.

In order to clean up the uranium for a mass apectrometric
analysis, 1t was subjected to another ether extraction as in
the first step. After the NH, NO was destroyed the sample was
fumed with HC10,, to destroy any organic resldue from the ether

extraction.

Plutonium Calculatlon:

To determine the amount of Pu in the origlnal sample, it
is necessary to measure ln a Frlsch chamber the alpha spectrum
of the plate prepared in Step 8. The ratio of Pu-239 and Pu-240
activity to Pu-236 activity is calculated. If the ratio is mul-
tiplied by the original activity of Pu-236 added, the original
activity of Pu-239 plus Pu-240 can be obtained. From the mass
analysis a Pu-239 to Pu-240 atom retio is obtained. The speci-
fic activity of the mixture ls calculated from that of the lndl-
vidual isotopes. The Pu-239 plus Pu-240 activity can be con-
verted to Pu=239 plus Pu-2U40 weight by dividing this activity
by the speclfilic activity of the mixture.

Uranium Calculation:

The ratio of the various U lsotopes to U-233 from the mass
spectrometer data 1s multiplled by the amount of U-233 splke
originally added to the sample to obtaln the amount of each

uranlum isotope present ln the orlglnal sample.

Source: W. J. Maeck, G. L. Booman, M. C. Elllott, and J. E.

Abstract

Uranium as tetrapropylammonium uranyl trinltrate is quan-

PROCEDURE 6: Determination of Uranium in Uranium Concentrates.

Source: R. J. Guest and J. B. Zimmerman, Anal. Chem. 27, 931 (1955).

Abstract
A method is described for the determination of uranium in high grade
uranium material. Uranlum ls separated from contaminants by means
of an ethyl acetate extraction using aluminum nitrate as a salting

agent. After the uranium has been stripped from the ethyl acetate

276
PROCEDURE 6 (Continued)

layer by means of water, colorimetric determination of the uranium
is carried out by the sodlum hydroxlde-hydrogen peroxide method.
The procedure 1s accurate, rapld, and easlly adeptable to routine

work.

Reagents and Apparatus

Reagents. Ethyl acetate (Merck, reagent grade).

ALUMINUM NITRATE SALTING SOLUTION. Place approximately 450
grams of reagent grade (Mallinckrodt) aluminum-nitrate [A1(NOg) 5
9H,,0] in a 600-ml. beaker and add 25 to 50 ml. of distilled water.
Cover the beaker and heat the mlxture on a hot plate. If a clear
solution does not result after 5 to 10 minutes of bolling, add 20
ml. of water, and continue the bolling for 5 more minutes. Repeat
this step untll a clear solution 1s obtalned after bolling. Remove
the cover glass and concentrate the solutlon by bolling untll a
boiling point of 130°C. 1s reached. Cover the beaker with a watch
glass and either transfer the solution to a constant temperature
apparatus or keep the solution warm, finally heating to just under
bolling before use. If the solutlon 1s allowed to cool to approxi-
mately 60°C., recrystallization of aluminum nitrate wlll take place.
It is necessary, therefore, to dllute the salting agent solution by
about one third ln order to prevent recrystallization 1f the solu-
tion cools to room temperature. Accordlngly, 1f the solution 1s to
stand overnight, add 35 ml. of dilstllled water per 100 ml. of salt-
ing agent solution, mlx well, and cover.

If the salting agent solutlon 1s. to be stored, the following
procedure has been found convenlent. Adjust the solution to the
proper concentration (boiling point, 130°C.) and transfer to a 100-
ml. three-necked reaction flask set on a heating mantle. AdJust the
heating eo that the temperature of the solutlon 1p kept at about
110°C. In one of the necks place a water condenser, in another neck

a thermometer, and in the third neck a removable ground-glass stop-

277
PROCEDURE. 6 (Continued)

per. This third neck 1s utilized for plpetting the salting agent
solution. |

ALUMINUM NITRATE WASH SOLUTION. Add 100 ml. of aluminum nitrate
salting solution (boiling point, 130°C.) to 73 ml. of distilled
water and 4 ml. of concentrated nitric acid.

Apparatus. Beckman DU speotrophotometer.

Heating mantle.

Three-necked reaction flask (1000 ml.).

Water condenser.

No. O rubber stoppers. Boll twlce in ethyl acetate before use.

1s to be calculated on a dry welght basis.

Bring the sample into solution In one of three ways: (1)

carbon-sodium peroxide fusion.

For the nltric acld treatment, dlssolve the sample in a
sultable quantity of nitric acld and transfer the solution and
insoluble residue Into an appropriate volumetric flask and make

for extraction wlll contaln between 10 and 30 mg. of uranium

be 250 ml. AdJust the acldlty of the sample solutlon to about
5% in nitric acid.

If nitric acld treatment 1s not sufficient, treat the
Sample wlth hydrochloric acld, nitrlc acid, perchloric acld,
and finally sulfuric acid. If necessary, add a few milliliters

PROCEDURE 6 (Continued)

of hydrofluoric acld. Fume the sample to dryness and leach
the resldue with nltrle acid, flnally transferring the solution
and resldue to an appropriate volumetric flask and adjusting

If the sample is refractory, use the sugar carbon-sodium

per 5-ml allquot of sample and standards before extraction. Where
